  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_b70adf16-effd-48e7-9b8e-5c9de76936ae'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer5569258043855463891">From the Pulitzer Prize–winning author of <i>March</i>, the journey of a rare illuminated manuscript through centuries of exile and war<br/><br/>In 1996, Hanna Heath, an Australian rare-book expert, is offered the job of a lifetime: analysis and conservation of the famed Sarajevo Haggadah, which has been rescued from Serb shelling during the Bosnian war. Priceless and beautiful, the book i</span>
  <span id="freeText5569258043855463891" style="display:none">From the Pulitzer Prize–winning author of <i>March</i>, the journey of a rare illuminated manuscript through centuries of exile and war<br /><br />In 1996, Hanna Heath, an Australian rare-book expert, is offered the job of a lifetime: analysis and conservation of the famed Sarajevo Haggadah, which has been rescued from Serb shelling during the Bosnian war. Priceless and beautiful, the book is one of the earliest Jewish volumes ever to be illuminated with images. When Hanna, a caustic loner with a passion for her work, discovers a series of tiny artifacts in its ancient binding—an insect wing fragment, wine stains, salt crystals, a white hair—she begins to unlock the book’s mysteries. The reader is ushered into an exquisitely detailed and atmospheric past, tracing the book’s journey from its salvation back to its creation. <br /><br />In Bosnia during World War II, a Muslim risks his life to protect it from the Nazis. In the hedonistic salons of fin-de-siècle Vienna, the book becomes a pawn in the struggle against the city’s rising anti-Semitism. In inquisition-era Venice, a Catholic priest saves it from burning. In Barcelona in 1492, the scribe who wrote the text sees his family destroyed by the agonies of enforced exile. And in Seville in 1480, the reason for the Haggadah’s extraordinary illuminations is finally disclosed. Hanna’s investigation unexpectedly plunges her into the intrigues of fine art forgers and ultra-nationalist fanatics. Her experiences will test her belief in herself and the man she has come to love. <br /><br />Inspired by a true story, <i>People of the Book</i> is at once a novel of sweeping historical grandeur and intimate emotional intensity, an ambitious, electrifying work by an acclaimed and beloved author.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5569258043855463891" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

    <div id=buyButtonContainer class="uitext buttons buyableFeature u-marginTopXSmall u-paddingBottomXSmall u-bottomGrayBorder" data-book-id=1379961 >

    <div class="u-positionRelative">

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">You should definitely read People of the Book by Geraldine Brooks. The amount of research evidenced by the cultural and historical details that so enr<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">You should definitely read People of the Book by Geraldine Brooks. The amount of research evidenced by the cultural and historical details that so enriched this book just astounds me. Brooks is a true master of fine literature. If you can find the book in audiobook form--I got it in CD book from my public library--do yourself a favor and "read" it that way. Hearing the difficult names pronounced added to my enjoyment of the book.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>

<div id="review_84909114" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/84909114" />
    <a title="Amanda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1219253-amanda"><img alt="Amanda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1411956191p2/1219253.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/84909114?book_show_action=true">Jan 13, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amanda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amanda" href="/user/show/1219253-amanda">Amanda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1219253-amanda?shelf=in-praise-of-books">in-praise-of-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1219253-amanda?shelf=book-club-made-me-do-it">book-club-made-me-do-it</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1219253-amanda?shelf=blog">blog</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer84909114" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15455751540199045824">A Review of <i>People of the Book</i> <br/>(or, Why I Hate the Kindle)<br/><br/>Brooks's novel is a fictionalized account of the real <i>Sarajevo Haggadah</i>, a Jewish religious text noteworthy for its inclusion of an illuminated manuscript and for its survival through turmoil and the hostility towards Jews that has erupted time and again over the centuries in Europe and Eastern Europe. The novel is told from the perspective of Hanna Heath, an expert in book restoration, who is called in to restore the text for display. Wh</span>
  <span id="freeText15455751540199045824" style="display:none">A Review of <i>People of the Book</i> <br />(or, Why I Hate the Kindle)<br /><br />Brooks's novel is a fictionalized account of the real <i>Sarajevo Haggadah</i>, a Jewish religious text noteworthy for its inclusion of an illuminated manuscript and for its survival through turmoil and the hostility towards Jews that has erupted time and again over the centuries in Europe and Eastern Europe. The novel is told from the perspective of Hanna Heath, an expert in book restoration, who is called in to restore the text for display. While working on the book, Hanna finds a few curiosities that she keeps and carefully labels: a butterfly wing, a small sample of some wine stained pages, salt crystals, a white hair, and the notation of some missing decorative clasps. As Hanna investigates each of these items and their origins to gain insight into the <i>Haggadah</i>'s past, the reader is presented with the story of each noteworthy item in its own stand alone chapter (stories that Hanna herself can never learn as the evidence she finds only provides her with a basis for conjecture and hypothesis). Each story is unique and not necessarily connected to the others. While the novel has been compared to <i>The Da Vinci Code</i>, it's a far cry from Robert Langdon's action-adventure chase through Europe in pursuit of an explosive secret that might change religion as we know it. Instead, the pacing is slower--the pacing of a scholar motivated by the desire to simply know, even if definitive answers aren't available. And, though the novel explores the nature of Jewish/Muslim/Christian relationships throughout the ages, it doesn't seek to lecture about morality or about what one should (or should not) believe.<br /><br />Despite enjoying it, I will admit that <i>People of the Book</i> has some flaws. The story of Hanna Heath and her strained relationship with her ultra-feminist, professional mother is cliched and not given enough room to become a realistic exploration of a such a complicated relationship. In addition, a few plot points are contrived, but I can forgive that simply because the book appealed to the book lover in me, which is a nice segue way into . . . WHY I HATE THE KINDLE (and all other eReader devices).<br /><br />First off, don't lecture me about how this is the future and I need to embrace it. If you own a Kindle, fine. Enjoy. I'm not suggesting that the privilege be taken away from you. However, I'll not be tempted by the siren song of fashionable technology. I love books. I love the way they feel. I like physically seeing the progress I've made as I turn page after page. I love the cover art. I love how books look on a shelf (in home decorating magazines, I delight in trying to identify the books on the shelves of well-appointed dens and studies). I like to select which books are going on vacation with me, agonizing over which ones might suit my mood. And, when I see someone reading a book, I will often become a creepy Peeping Tom of sorts as I try to catch a glimpse of the book cover so I can see what they're reading. I judge you by what book you're reading--if you're reading Neil Gaiman, I want to know you; conversely, if you're reading <i>Twilight</i>, I may be silently hoping that you get to join the undead (but in a more permanent dead sort of way). So much of that is lost with an eReader. And, after reading <i>People of the Book</i>, I'm aware of how much history can be lost. Not just the tiny fragments that get wedged into the bindings and between the pages, but the history of the people who owned and cherished the book. A world where physical books become obsolete and everyone has an entire library on one portable reading device is also a frightening possibility. How easy then for the next dictator to destroy our beloved texts. Smash one eReader and hundreds, thousands of books are permanently lost--far more efficient than book burnings. It's the impermanence of it all that scares me. Not only that, I think that obsession with books, recognizing and identifying with others because you notice the Christopher Moore font on the book cover or the tell-tell cover art of a Tim O'Brien paperback, helps create a reading community that we're connected to and a part of. How many chance encounters, spontaneous conversations, or just the simple nod of respect to complete strangers with whom we briefly feel connected when we realize we're reading the same author on the same bus--how many of those moments are lost when we're all carrying around the same reading device that indicates no individuality or reading preference to those around us? Will we feel as open to asking a complete stranger, "What are you reading?" Obviously, not all books are as important as the <i>Haggadah</i>, but I like to think that we all cherish our own quaint libraries and someday perhaps they will tell the world about who we were.<br /><br />Cross posted at <a href="http://viiamanda.blogspot.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">This Insignificant Cinder</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="15455751540199045824" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_44296438" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/44296438" />
    <a title="Catherine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1392534-catherine"><img alt="Catherine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1218595127p2/1392534.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/44296438?book_show_action=true">Jan 25, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Catherine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Catherine" href="/user/show/1392534-catherine">Catherine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143115007" href="/book/show/4027239-people-of-the-book">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1392534-catherine?shelf=2009">2009</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1392534-catherine?shelf=bosnia">bosnia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1392534-catherine?shelf=mainstream-us">mainstream-us</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1392534-catherine?shelf=australia">australia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1392534-catherine?shelf=spain">spain</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1392534-catherine?shelf=judaism">judaism</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer44296438" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9826808566949953093">This is an awful book.<br/><br/>I expected great things from Brooks - <i>March</i> is a book I treasure - but this novel is a third-rate Da Vinci code, written with about the same amount of skill.<br/><br/>The premise is captivating - a 500-year-old haggadah is found in Sarajevo in 1996, and the novel sets out to explore the book's journey across Europe in those intervening years. Along the way, the haggadah acts as an entry point into the tumult, crisis, and unspeakable violence experienced by Jewish communities across E</span>
  <span id="freeText9826808566949953093" style="display:none">This is an awful book.<br /><br />I expected great things from Brooks - <i>March</i> is a book I treasure - but this novel is a third-rate Da Vinci code, written with about the same amount of skill.<br /><br />The premise is captivating - a 500-year-old haggadah is found in Sarajevo in 1996, and the novel sets out to explore the book's journey across Europe in those intervening years. Along the way, the haggadah acts as an entry point into the tumult, crisis, and unspeakable violence experienced by Jewish communities across Europe. <br /><br />Yet the novel does not live up to the premise. The focus is not upon the haggadah or the people who have handled it between 1480 and 1996, but rather upon the Australian conservator called in to restore it in Sarajevo. The details of where the haggadah has been are important because Hanna, the conservator, is writing an essay about its journey, and she'll gain academic and professional prestige from doing so. Hello, cultural appropriation! For example: "why had an illuminator working in Spain, for a Jewish client, in the manner of a European Christian, have used an Iranian paintbrush? Clarissa's identification of this anomaly had been great for my essay. It had given me an excuse to riff on the way knowledge had traveled amazing distances during the <i>Conveivencia</i>, over well-established routes linking the artists and intellectuals of Spain with their counterparts in Baghdad, Cairo, and Isphahan." (321) (We are actually supposed to clap our hands with glee on Hanna's behalf at that point, I think.) Once Hanna's expertise about the haggadah is questioned, she gives up her work as a conservator of old, European and Middle Eastern texts, and instead starts saving Australian Aboriginal art from being destroyed by mining companies. She has an assistant - he's Aboriginal, but it's Hanna who we're supposed to identify and sympathize with, feeling pleased that she's a white superwoman, saving people from themselves.<br /><br />There are other truly problematic issues of race in the text. The first character of color we encounter is a Rasta cab driver who smokes ganja and who won't drop her at Scotland Yard in case he gets caught for using drugs. We meet a man - Raz - who is part African-American and part Hawaiian, and whom the protagonist observes "was one of those vanguard beings of indeterminate ethnicity, the magnificent mutts I hope we are all destined to become given another millennium of intermixing." (141). Yep, that's right, she just called him a mongrel. The depictions of Jewish, Muslim, and Christian faith are so broad-brushed i don't know what to think - it's like a child's paint-by-numbers for major world religions.<br /><br />And of course, in the tradition of Dan Brown, it's a love story. Within a few pages of beginning the book Hanna's sleeping with the Muslim curator of Sarajevo's major museum, and by the end she's overcome her aversion to the idea of a long-term relationship and is ready to be with him. Whoop-dee-doo. <br /><br />In conclusion: UGH.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9826808566949953093" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1491823211" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1491823211" />
    <a title="Brina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2933855-brina"><img alt="Brina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601675085p2/2933855.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1491823211?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brina" href="/user/show/2933855-brina">Brina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=jewish-books">jewish-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2933855-brina?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1491823211" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15389531667301557330">A tip from one of my daughter's teachers lead me to the works of Geraldine Brooks, a two time recipient of the Pulitzer Prize for literature. Being the non-fiction connoisseur that I am, I first devoured her memoir Foreign Correspondence. Deciding not to limit myself to only one of her books, I chose People of the Book, her fictionalized history of the Sarajevo Haggadah. <br/><br/>Hanna Heath is a Sydney book conservator who has been chosen by the Sarajevo National Museum to rebind the city's famous Hagga</span>
  <span id="freeText15389531667301557330" style="display:none">A tip from one of my daughter's teachers lead me to the works of Geraldine Brooks, a two time recipient of the Pulitzer Prize for literature. Being the non-fiction connoisseur that I am, I first devoured her memoir Foreign Correspondence. Deciding not to limit myself to only one of her books, I chose People of the Book, her fictionalized history of the Sarajevo Haggadah. <br /><br />Hanna Heath is a Sydney book conservator who has been chosen by the Sarajevo National Museum to rebind the city's famous Haggadah in anticipation of the museum's reopening. Spurred on by her research, Heath's travels take her to Harvard, London, Vienna, and back to Sarajevo later on in order to pinpoint the codex's travels. If the book only centered on Heath's quest in present day, it would still merit a five-star book of intrigue. How fortunate that this is not the case. <br /><br />Brooks intersperses Heath's quest to discover the haggadah's and her own history with chapters on each of the haggadah's stops over the last 500 years. As Heath discovers artifacts while researching, Brooks takes us back in time to World II Sarajevo, Vienna, Florence, and pre-inquisition Spain. The pages are filled with vivid language each describing an epoch of the haggadah's illustrious history. <br />Of course being fiction, Brooks ties up both Hanna's and the haggadah's loose ends with a relative happy ending. I grew more and more mesmerized with the books twists and turns, and the pages read quickly in the book's second half. Where would Heath's quest lead next? Read on and discover the haggadah's path through history. <br /><br />As someone who rarely reads fiction, I am delighted with my choice of both book and author as the one to lead me back to the world of storytelling. Brooks' writing is first rate and I look forward to reading many more of her novels. I would highly recommend this book to anyone in search of a quality historical fiction novel.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15389531667301557330" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

<span id="freeTextContainer3022036760460735051"><i>What I do is me, for that I came</i>...<br/><br/>This is grand book. Impressive. Intriguing. Tragic. Beautiful. From beginning to the end.<br/>I don't usually like books on war situations but this book received so many good comments and ratings from Goodreads I decided to go for it. I did not regret it.<br/><br/>Each chapter is a time jump, to and fro in time. And starts with a quote, like this one, page 329 in my book:<br/> <i>A white hair<br/>Seville, 1480<br/>My eyes seep sorrow; water skins with holes<br/>- Abid bin al-Abras</i><br/><br/>Part of a review (</span>
  <span id="freeText3022036760460735051" style="display:none"><i>What I do is me, for that I came</i>...<br /><br />This is grand book. Impressive. Intriguing. Tragic. Beautiful. From beginning to the end.<br />I don't usually like books on war situations but this book received so many good comments and ratings from Goodreads I decided to go for it. I did not regret it.<br /><br />Each chapter is a time jump, to and fro in time. And starts with a quote, like this one, page 329 in my book:<br /> <i>A white hair<br />Seville, 1480<br />My eyes seep sorrow; water skins with holes<br />- Abid bin al-Abras</i><br /><br />Part of a review (Miami Herald): "Stellar - compelling story. Brooks seamlessly moves from the miniscule - the tiny specks - to examine in human terms the larger events from the thirteenth century and into the twenty-first: the inquisition, the rise of anti-semitism, nazism and the holocaust, religious wars and forces exiles, in Bosnia, Venice, Barcelona and Seville."<br /><br />Big five star. A sensitive story, crossing borders, crossing time lines....Realistic and poetic at the same time. <br />Will be back with more, probably in the weekend. <br />Highly recommended!<br /><br /><i>I wanted to give a sense of the people of the book, the different hands that had made it, used it, protected it....</i></span>

You can join the discussion at <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/'>https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/</a>... <br/><br/>People of the Book by Geraldine Brooks<br/><br/>Most people who like books will enjoy a story about a “book detective” – a female Australian book curator/restorer who discovers many cultural and microscopic mysteries when she is hired to restore a Hagga</span>
  <span id="freeText937390440692285091" style="display:none">I edited this book review and added some photos to this older review because this book is being discussed in January 2020 by the GR Diversity in All Forms Book Club led by Mariah Roze. You can join the discussion at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/">https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/</a>... <br><br>People of the Book by Geraldine Brooks<br><br>Most people who like books will enjoy a story about a “book detective” – a female Australian book curator/restorer who discovers many cultural and microscopic mysteries when she is hired to restore a Haggadah, a book of prayers used during the Jewish Passover seder. The author takes us back in history see explanations that the restorer can only guess at. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1578097025i/28707676._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>As you might expect, it’s a tale of both beauty and horror ranging from the 1400’s to the present. The book restoration is taking place in Sarajevo and the protagonist gets involved with a Bosnian who is taking care of his son who was wounded during the conflict. We learn of that area’s recent grim history. <br><br>Added to this is the book restorer’s bitter and embattled relationship with her mother (I think I saw them on Jerry Springer) leading to the revelation that she herself is Jewish. <br><br>So we have four story themes: book detective, mother-daughter feud, happenings in modern Bosnia, and the bulk of the book: the Jewish diaspora starting with the Spanish Inquisition. It is quite well knit together. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1578097025i/28707677._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="275" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>We also get very insightful reading of the landscapes (I say this as a geography professor) of Bosnia, Boston, London and Australia.<br><br>Photo of a Haggadah from judaicawebstore.com<br>Photo of the author from prhspeakers.com</span>

  <span id="freeText5675005786902229633" style="display:none">An exceptional novel about a rare book conservator from Australia who researches the Sarajevo Haggadda, an ancient Jewish prayer book. <br><br>Brooks uses the protagonist's research to tell the story of the book backwards from WWII to 1600s Venice to Moorish Spain. The modern conservators narrative binds the vignettes together. <br><br>A none too subtle vehicle to highlight the interwoven histories of Christians, Jews and Muslims - the People of the Book - the novel is also an allegory about learning itself and people's struggles to keep the flame of wisdom alight. <br><br>Original, well researched and provocative, a reader will enjoy the textured characterizations and the personality brimming in each historical sketch.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1517800386i/25004310.jpg" width="196" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>

<div id="review_1866513605" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1866513605" />
    <a title="Hannah Greendale" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43377498-hannah-greendale"><img alt="Hannah Greendale" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1466436186p2/43377498.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1866513605?book_show_action=true">Jan 04, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hannah Greendale" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hannah Greendale" href="/user/show/43377498-hannah-greendale">Hannah Greendale</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43377498-hannah-greendale?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1866513605" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6495312605125066372">A Hebrew manuscript created in fifteenth-century Spain has been saved from the ruins of a bombed library. Hanna Heath, who specializes in the conservation of medieval documents, is hired to repair and preserve the ancient manuscript. Tiny artifacts found inside the manuscript lead Hanna on a quest to discover how the rare manuscript was created and who risked everything to ensure its safety for five hundred years. <br/><br/>The author capitalizes on Hanna's passion for her profession. Her work on the manu</span>
  <span id="freeText6495312605125066372" style="display:none">A Hebrew manuscript created in fifteenth-century Spain has been saved from the ruins of a bombed library. Hanna Heath, who specializes in the conservation of medieval documents, is hired to repair and preserve the ancient manuscript. Tiny artifacts found inside the manuscript lead Hanna on a quest to discover how the rare manuscript was created and who risked everything to ensure its safety for five hundred years. <br /><br />The author capitalizes on Hanna's passion for her profession. Her work on the manuscript is described with such alluring detail that the reader cannot help but experience the same hushed reverence as she does. When Hanna looks at the manuscript, she sees more than paper and ink. She sees the story behind the book's creation; she senses the hands of every person who made it, held it, cherished it. What others see as blemishes or trash - a red stain, a salt crystal, a white hair lost in the folds of the binding - Hanna sees as clues to the people of the book. <br /><br />Hanna's story alone is strong enough to carry the reader through a captivating journey, but what makes this book so beguiling is the integration of multiple stories from various other characters spanning from 1480 to 2002. All of the varied narratives are masterfully woven together for optimal plot pacing. In a way, the book reads like a collection of short stories, but a common thread - the ancient manuscript - ties everything together into one beautiful tapestry. <br /><br />While <i>People of the Book</i> doesn't offer the same richness of prose as the author's other novels, there are moments where dazzling language emerges. Often this language is employed to give an intimate, artful description of the manuscript itself, such that <i>People of the Book</i> sometimes feels like a love letter to the act of slowly crafting a masterpiece: <br /><br /><i>Blue: intense as a midsummer sky, obtained from grinding precious lapis lazuli carried by camel caravan all the way from the mountains of Afghanistan. White: pure, creamy, opaque. [. . . ] There was yellow made of saffron. That beautiful autumnal flower,</i> Crocus sativus Linnaeous<i>, each with just three tiny precious sigmas, had been a prized luxury then and remained one, still.</i> <br /><br />Other times, the writing is vivid and immediate:<br /><br /><i>[He] continued speaking quietly, in short, undramatic sentences. No light. A fractured pipe. Rising water. Shells hitting the walls. It was left for me to fill in the blanks. I'd been in enough museum basements to imagine how it was; how every shell burst that shook the building must have sent a rain of plaster falling over the precious things, and over him, too, into his eyes as he crouched in the dark, hands shaking, striking match after match to see what he was doing.</i> <br /><br />Fundamental themes woven throughout the book are as provocative and meaningful today as they were five hundred years ago: <br /><br /><i>"I have spent many nights, lying awake here in this room, thinking that the [manuscript] came to Sarajevo for a reason. It was here to test us, to see if there were people who could see that what unites us was more than what divided us. That to be a human being matters more than to be a Jew or a Muslim, Catholic or Orthodox."</i> <br /><br />With rich character narratives and a deep veneration for artifacts, <i>People of the Book</i> sweeps readers away on a grand, emotional adventure that crosses the globe and spans centuries. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6495312605125066372" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_14727750" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/14727750" />
    <a title="Leanna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/335117-leanna"><img alt="Leanna" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/14727750?book_show_action=true">Feb 06, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leanna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leanna" href="/user/show/335117-leanna">Leanna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer14727750" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer612997951072534766">I try to avoid all things popular (e.g., I’ve never seen <i>Star Wars</i> or <i>Titanic</i>) because I know, after all the hype, I can only be disappointed. When it comes to books, though, I feel obligated to read what’s popular so I can participate somewhat intelligently in the conversation.<br/><br/>That being said, although I hoped Geraldine Brook’s <i>People of the Book</i> would live up to the buzz, I wasn’t too surprised when it did not. The book is good, but it is not call-up-all-my-friends-(or readers)-and-recommend-i</span>
  <span id="freeText612997951072534766" style="display:none">I try to avoid all things popular (e.g., I’ve never seen <i>Star Wars</i> or <i>Titanic</i>) because I know, after all the hype, I can only be disappointed. When it comes to books, though, I feel obligated to read what’s popular so I can participate somewhat intelligently in the conversation.<br /><br />That being said, although I hoped Geraldine Brook’s <i>People of the Book</i> would live up to the buzz, I wasn’t too surprised when it did not. The book is good, but it is not call-up-all-my-friends-(or readers)-and-recommend-it good.<br /><br /><i>People</i> has been compared to <i>The Da Vinci Code</i>, but I find that comparison erroneous. Although better written than <i>Da Vinci</i> (but, come on, a phonebook is better written than <i>Da Vinci</i>), <i>People</i> lacks the plot, mystery, and pizzazz that made <i>Da Vinci</i> a blockbuster.<br /><br />Instead, <i>People</i> is much more reminiscent of Susan Vreeland’s <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/321577.Girl_in_Hyacinth_Blue" title="Girl in Hyacinth Blue by Susan Vreeland" rel="nofollow noopener">Girl in Hyacinth Blue</a>
</i>. <i>Hyacinth</i> follows the provenance of a Vermeer painting. <i>People</i> follows the provenance of the Sarajevo Haggadah.<br /><br />As such, the book is divided into several sections. Five sections follow the Haggadah back in history—to Bosnia, to Austria-Hungary, to Italy, to Spain. As the title suggests, it is not the book that is interesting so much as what happens in the lives of those people attached to it.<br /><br />These sections are the strongest and most interesting in the book. However, for some reason I cannot fathom, some parts are written in first person and some in third. This twist seems to serve little purpose other than to distract and annoy the reader.<br /><br />The book’s greatest weakness is the contemporary storyline that cushions each section. Hanna Heath is a book conservator hired to work on the Haggadah. She finds clues in the book—an insect, a stain, a hair—that reveal its history.<br /><br />Unfortunately, I found Hanna’s story to be downright irritating. Hanna is 30 years old, has a double bachelor’s degree, a master’s degree, and a PhD. She has apprenticed around the globe, is well published and highly regarded in her field. Perhaps I am just jealous, since Hanna and I share the same age and similar academic credentials (okay, hers are much better than mine), but Hanna’s experience and success is simply not plausible for someone so young.<br /><br />Similarly, everyone Hanna meets—from Vienna’s chief archivist to Sarajevo’s head museum librarian—is 30 or under. Really? How did Hanna and her cohorts pack in so much and become so successful in so few years?<br /><br />I could continue my nitpickiness (Ozren, the head librarian, speaks flawless English but stumbles over the word “hoof”?), but the point is that Hanna is so unbelievable she becomes a rather unsympathetic character. I was far more interested in what happens when she is out of the picture.<br /><br /><i>People of the Book</i> is an okay read, but I see no need to trample your friends and neighbors to secure a copy. Read it if you have the time and inclination. If not . . . </span>
  <a data-text-id="612997951072534766" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

I adored Geraldine Brook's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4965.Year_of_Wonders" title="Year of Wonders by Geraldine Brooks" rel="nofollow noopener">Year of Wonders</a> and I thought this would be an easy hit for me.<br /><br />I think the problem is fairly simple- never connected with the main character. Again, I can't believe I didn't like this.<br /><br />Highly recommend <i>Year of Wonders</i>. I give this a solid pass.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer16654719982219398589">There's nothing bad about this but there's nothing exciting about it either. I'd describe it as assembly line fiction. A novel that is designed to be a crowd pleaser. It never strays from formulaic commercial boundaries. The story is well-plotted and researched. The prose is professional but never inspired. <div id="review_1704859419" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1704859419" />
    <a title="PattyMacDotComma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1128612-pattymacdotcomma"><img alt="PattyMacDotComma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1577835191p2/1128612.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1704859419?book_show_action=true">Jul 21, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="PattyMacDotComma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="PattyMacDotComma" href="/user/show/1128612-pattymacdotcomma">PattyMacDotComma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1128612-pattymacdotcomma?shelf=award-win-listed">award-win-listed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1128612-pattymacdotcomma?shelf=australian-author">australian-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1128612-pattymacdotcomma?shelf=aa">aa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1128612-pattymacdotcomma?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1128612-pattymacdotcomma?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1128612-pattymacdotcomma?shelf=favourites-adult">favourites-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1704859419" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6131659557827615417"><b>5★ </b><br/>A new favourite! I love it when old stories sound right for their time, and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/211268.Geraldine_Brooks' title='Geraldine Brooks' rel='nofollow noopener'>Geraldine Brooks</a> does that so well. This novel was inspired by the discovery of the real <i>Sarajevo Haggadah</i>, a book more than 700 years old, so Brooks had a lot of ground to cover and a lot of voices to invent.<br/><br/>Her central character (Hanna Heath, a rare book expert) says about herself: <br/><br/><b>“By linking research and imagination, sometimes I can think myself into the heads of the people who made the book. I can figure out who </b></span>
  <span id="freeText6131659557827615417" style="display:none"><b>5★ </b><br />A new favourite! I love it when old stories sound right for their time, and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/211268.Geraldine_Brooks" title="Geraldine Brooks" rel="nofollow noopener">Geraldine Brooks</a> does that so well. This novel was inspired by the discovery of the real <i>Sarajevo Haggadah</i>, a book more than 700 years old, so Brooks had a lot of ground to cover and a lot of voices to invent.<br /><br />Her central character (Hanna Heath, a rare book expert) says about herself: <br /><br /><b>“By linking research and imagination, sometimes I can think myself into the heads of the people who made the book. I can figure out who they were, or how they worked. That’s how I add my few grains to the sandbox of human knowledge. It’s what I love best about what I do.”</b><br /><br />That describes exactly what I enjoy about what Brooks does--imagines plausible, informative, entertaining scenarios.<br /><br />Who might have penned the text of the haggadah (the Jewish ritual which is read during the Passover Seder meal)? Who might have painted the illustrations? Who is the black woman in a painting, and why is she there? Who managed to save this Jewish treasure from the Spanish Inquisition, the Nazis, and the numerous attempts to eradicate all traces of Jewish culture? <br /><br />Hanna says about the paper she plans to write: <br /><br /><b>“I wanted this one to be different. I wanted to give a sense of the people of the book, the different hands that had made it, used it, protected it. I wanted it to be a gripping narrative, even suspenseful.”</b><br /><br />This novel is all of those things. We hold our breath as this small book changes hands on its precarious journey through the centuries. Sometimes it’s a Muslim, sometimes a Christian, sometimes a Polish orphan fleeing Nazis in the winter snow. All are in desperately dangerous situations.<br /><br />This has everything--childbirth, torture, intrigue, power, war, greed, compassion--and Hanna’s own story as well.<br /><br />In 1996, Dr Hanna Heath (PhD doctor), an expert Australian conservator of rare books, is invited to Sarajevo to inspect a recently discovered haggadah [Brooks does not capitalise it, incidentally]. It just turned up in the museum, and the unusual illustrations have roused particular interest, as images were generally forbidden in Jewish tradition.<br /><br />Hanna spots the tiniest clues, hoping to be <b>“lucky enough to find any debris in the binding—it’s amazing what you can learn about a book by studying the chemistry of a bread crumb.”</b><br /><br />Rather than a crumb, she finds a hair and some stains and some missing clasps. <br /><br />Hanna’s methods are as fascinating as the results of her investigation. And there is humour, too.<br /><br /><b>“The art world in England is an absolute magnet for the second sons of threadbare lords, or women named Annabelle Something-hyphen-Something who dress in black leggings and burnt orange cashmeres and smell faintly of wet Labrador. I always find myself lapsing into Paleolithic Strine when I’m around them, using words I’d never dream of using in real life, like<i> ‘cobber’</i> and <i>‘bonza’</i>. "</b><br /><br />Meanwhile, her mother, not only a ‘real’ doctor, but a neurosurgeon <b>(“I don’t just save lives, Hanna. I save the very thing that makes us human”</b>) is dismissive of Hanna’s cultural conservation expertise. She is a driven, single-minded woman, whose child was a puzzling nuisance. <br /><br />Hanna stands up to mum and uncovers her own history—where she came from and how—and she finds a whole new world of support and respect. <br /><br />But professionally, the rug is pulled out from under her when her expertise about the <i>Sarajevo Haggadah</i> is questioned, so she retreats back home to explore the world’s oldest culture instead. She’s embarrassed to discover how ignorant she (and everyone else) is.<br /><br /><b>“So I set myself a crash course and became a pioneer in a new field: desperation conservation.” </b><br /><br />Great term, and we need more of it. Desperation Conservation.<br /><br />She tries to put the haggadah story behind her and sets out to catalogue and preserve Aboriginal rock art in situ (diverting dripping water away from it, that sort of thing), working as fast as possible in the tropical heat in Arnhem Land, with little communication and limited time before The Wet turns the dirt tracks to impassable mud.<br /><br />So little is known, so little is protected. When she’s called to the Foreign Affairs office in Canberra from the Arnhem Land cave she’s working in, an official mentions he’s never been to the Top End. <br /><br /><b>“Typical, I thought. Probably been to every museum in Florence and yet never seen the Lightning Man at Nourlangie Rock.”</b><br /><br />Still, the <i>Sarajevo Haggadah</i> intrigues her, and she feels compelled to agree to the request to go back and face some unanswered questions. As her colleague says:<br /><br /><b>“I have spent many nights, lying awake here in this room, thinking that the haggadah came to Sarajevo for a reason. It was here to test us, to see if there were people who could see that what united us was more than what divided us. That to be a human being matters more than to be a Jew or a Muslim, Catholic or Orthodox.”</b><br /><br />What wonderful stories Brooks weaves around the facts. All believable, all fascinating. <br /><br />Takeaway message? <br /><br /><b>That to be a human being matters more than to be a Jew or a Muslim, Catholic or Orthodox.”</b><br /><br />The real book:<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sarajevo_Haggadah">https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sarajev...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="6131659557827615417" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_21226982" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/21226982" />
    <a title="Mary" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/858930-mary"><img alt="Mary" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/21226982?book_show_action=true">Apr 28, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mary" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mary" href="/user/show/858930-mary">Mary</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer21226982" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5608562588995737680">What a fantastic story. Don't be put off by the first bit when you meet Hanna, the main character: she is supposed to be kind of annoying. You end up loving her with a compassion that this author can magically instill in you for all of her characters, of which there are many. The book also spans many centuries and traverses many continents, so it's a bit complex. But wow: This account of the history of a little book takes you through the darkest hours of human history, including the Inquisition </span>
  <span id="freeText5608562588995737680" style="display:none">What a fantastic story. Don't be put off by the first bit when you meet Hanna, the main character: she is supposed to be kind of annoying. You end up loving her with a compassion that this author can magically instill in you for all of her characters, of which there are many. The book also spans many centuries and traverses many continents, so it's a bit complex. But wow: This account of the history of a little book takes you through the darkest hours of human history, including the Inquisition and the Holocaust, but you end up feeling somehow so uplifted by the story. Brooks has a way of taking dire, nasty topics (the bubonic plague and civil war are the settings of her other two novels)and turning them into parables that portray our deep goodness and strength and grace in the face of unimaginable horror and hardhship. I just think she is an amazingly gifted writer. Highly recommend. But not for the faint-hearted. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5608562588995737680" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_520100981" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/520100981" />
    <a title="Dorie  - Cats&amp;Books :)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16912659-dorie---cats-books"><img alt="Dorie  - Cats&amp;Books :)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1550077760p2/16912659.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/520100981?book_show_action=true">Jan 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dorie  - Cats&amp;Books :)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dorie  - Cats&amp;Books :)" href="/user/show/16912659-dorie---cats-books">Dorie  - Cats&amp;Books :)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16912659-dorie---cats-books?shelf=all-time-favorite">all-time-favorite</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16912659-dorie---cats-books?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16912659-dorie---cats-books?shelf=mystery-thriller">mystery-thriller</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer520100981" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8580531118118044212">Oh how I love Geraldine Brooks! This book is I think her masterpiece, so incredibly well researched and detailed.<br/><br/>In 1996, Hanna Heath, an Australian rare book expert is offered the job of a lifetime: analysis and conservation of the famed Sarajevo Haggadah, which has been rescued from Serb shelling during the Bosnian war. Priceless and Beautiful, the book is one of the earliest Jewish volumes ever to be illuminated with images. When Hanna, a caustic loner with a passion for her work, discovers a</span>
  <span id="freeText8580531118118044212" style="display:none">Oh how I love Geraldine Brooks! This book is I think her masterpiece, so incredibly well researched and detailed.<br /><br />In 1996, Hanna Heath, an Australian rare book expert is offered the job of a lifetime: analysis and conservation of the famed Sarajevo Haggadah, which has been rescued from Serb shelling during the Bosnian war. Priceless and Beautiful, the book is one of the earliest Jewish volumes ever to be illuminated with images. When Hanna, a caustic loner with a passion for her work, discovers a series of tiny artifacts in the book's ancient binding--an insect wing fragment, wine stains, salt crystals, a white hair--she begins to unlock the book's mysteries. The reader is ushered into an exquisitely detailed and atmospheric past, tracing the book's journey from it's salvation back to it's creation.<br /><br />In Bosnia during WWII, a Muslim risks his life to protect the book from the Nazis. In the hedonistic salons of fin-de-siecle Vienna, the book becomes a pawn in the struggle against the city's rising anti-Semitism. In inquisition-era Venice, a Catholic priest saves it from burning. <br /><br />In Barcelona in 1492, the scribe who wrote the text sees his family destroyed by the agonies of enforced exile. In Seville in 1480, the reason for the Haggadah's extraordinary illuminations is finally disclosed. Hanna's investigation unexpectedly plunges her into the intrigues of fine art forgers and ultra-nationalist fanatics. Her experiences will test her belief in herself and the man she has come to love.<br /><br />Inspired by a true story, "People of the Book" is at once a novel of sweeping historical grandeur and an intimate emotional intensity, an ambitious, electrifying work by an acclaimed and beloved author.<br /><br />I loved "Year of Wonders" but I now think that this book is Brook's masterpiece. I loved all of it and am glad that I started out writing down all of the names, it helped greatly throughout the novel.<br /><br />Highly recommend for any lover of fine literature and historical fiction. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8580531118118044212" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_36775399" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/36775399" />
    <a title="Diane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/279256-diane"><img alt="Diane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1497365060p2/279256.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/36775399?book_show_action=true">Mar 03, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diane" href="/user/show/279256-diane">Diane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/279256-diane?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer36775399" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11544460514835199525">This is a sweeping work of historical fiction, with characters starting in Sarajevo in 1996 and then slowly going back 500 years into the past. <br/><br/>The story is framed around Hanna, a rare book expert from Australia who is called in to analyze a precious Jewish text that was recovered during the Bosnian war. As Hanna studies the ancient book, she finds clues about its history, such as wine stains, a white hair, and part of an insect's wing. Each chapter takes the reader farther back in time, and we </span>
  <span id="freeText11544460514835199525" style="display:none">This is a sweeping work of historical fiction, with characters starting in Sarajevo in 1996 and then slowly going back 500 years into the past. <br /><br />The story is framed around Hanna, a rare book expert from Australia who is called in to analyze a precious Jewish text that was recovered during the Bosnian war. As Hanna studies the ancient book, she finds clues about its history, such as wine stains, a white hair, and part of an insect's wing. Each chapter takes the reader farther back in time, and we see how many miracles it took to preserve the book for so long.<br /><br />Being a librarian and a general lover of books, I was intrigued by this novel and expected to love it. It is a good story, but my enjoyment was dampened by the melodramatic dialogue — especially the overwrought relationship of Hanna with her mother — and some of the historical scenes were a bit too precious for my taste. <br /><br />It is an interesting story and it's obvious that Geraldine Brooks did a tremendous amount of research to write it. I admire the work that went into creating this novel, and do plan to read more of Geraldine's work.<br /><br /><b>Opening Passage</b><br />"I might as well say, right from the jump: it wasn't my usual kind of job. I like to work alone, in my own clean, silent, well-lit laboratory, where the climate is controlled and everything I need is right at hand. It's true that I have developed a reputation as someone who can work effectively out of the lab, when I have to, when the museums don't want to pay the travel insurance on a piece, or when private collectors don't want anyone to know exactly what it is that they own. It's also true that I've flown halfway around the world, to do an interesting job. But never to a place like this: the boardroom of a bank in the middle of a city where they just stopped shooting at each other five minutes ago."</span>
  <a data-text-id="11544460514835199525" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer101526736" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3323251317727119725">This is a wonderful story of a magical book, an illuminated manuscript begun in the 15th century and found in Sarajevo after the Bosnian War, a Jewish manuscript rescued by a Muslim librarian who could not bear to see such a treasure be destroyed.<br/><br/>Based on some fact and the author's talented recreation, we see the history of this religious piece over the years as some seek to destroy it and others work to save or embellish it. We move backward in time from the modern time to the Nazi era, to 19th</span>
  <span id="freeText3323251317727119725" style="display:none">This is a wonderful story of a magical book, an illuminated manuscript begun in the 15th century and found in Sarajevo after the Bosnian War, a Jewish manuscript rescued by a Muslim librarian who could not bear to see such a treasure be destroyed.<br /><br />Based on some fact and the author's talented recreation, we see the history of this religious piece over the years as some seek to destroy it and others work to save or embellish it. We move backward in time from the modern time to the Nazi era, to 19th century Europe, to the days of the Inquisition. I found I learned some history here while following the book. For this I thank Ms Brooks. I definitely will have to try others of her books.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer26018485" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12881188573913020508">Geraldine Brook's latest is a treat for us librarians (as well as dedicated to us in the front!) as she traces the path of an ancient religious text that, although Jewish in origin, was saved and added to several times over by members of different religions and cultures throughout time. As usual, Brooks' prose is both incredibly readable and laudably literary, and her theme that the love of knowledge and books crosses all historical and cultural boundaries is well illustrated through her complex</span>
  <span id="freeText12881188573913020508" style="display:none">Geraldine Brook's latest is a treat for us librarians (as well as dedicated to us in the front!) as she traces the path of an ancient religious text that, although Jewish in origin, was saved and added to several times over by members of different religions and cultures throughout time. As usual, Brooks' prose is both incredibly readable and laudably literary, and her theme that the love of knowledge and books crosses all historical and cultural boundaries is well illustrated through her complex characterizations and real-life-inspired plot. In the end, all of us who love, teach, and support literacy and learning are "People of the Book." </span>

<div id="review_12304579" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/12304579" />
    <a title="Lisa Vegan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83445-lisa-vegan"><img alt="Lisa Vegan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1243394822p2/83445.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/12304579?book_show_action=true">Jan 11, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa Vegan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa Vegan" href="/user/show/83445-lisa-vegan">Lisa Vegan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83445-lisa-vegan?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83445-lisa-vegan?shelf=books-about-books">books-about-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83445-lisa-vegan?shelf=novel">novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83445-lisa-vegan?shelf=bookclub">bookclub</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83445-lisa-vegan?shelf=readbooks-female-author-or-illust">readbooks-female-author-or-illust</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83445-lisa-vegan?shelf=1-also-at-librarything">1-also-at-librarything</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83445-lisa-vegan?shelf=z2009">z2009</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83445-lisa-vegan?shelf=goodreads-author">goodreads-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83445-lisa-vegan?shelf=zz-5star">zz-5star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83445-lisa-vegan?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer12304579" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17185529907210619829">This is a marvelous book. I really enjoyed this author’s <u>Year of Wonders</u> and I think I liked this book at least as much. This is skillful and enthralling storytelling that’s also thought provoking.<br/><br/>This book is one of the most skillful renderings of a book that goes back and forth in time that I’ve ever read. Ditto for the writing of a historical fiction account, especially one that has part of its history in the very recent past.<br/><br/>This is a historical fiction story about the Sarajevo Haggadah. (A </span>
  <span id="freeText17185529907210619829" style="display:none">This is a marvelous book. I really enjoyed this author’s <u>Year of Wonders</u> and I think I liked this book at least as much. This is skillful and enthralling storytelling that’s also thought provoking.<br /><br />This book is one of the most skillful renderings of a book that goes back and forth in time that I’ve ever read. Ditto for the writing of a historical fiction account, especially one that has part of its history in the very recent past.<br /><br />This is a historical fiction story about the Sarajevo Haggadah. (A Haggadah is a Jewish religious text that sets out the order of the Passover Seder. During Pesach, it is read every year on the first night, and in some traditions on the second night as well, for the Seder service.)<br /><br />The story goes from present to past, back to present, to farther and farther in the past several times, each time returning to present day, and ending back again in the present. <br /><br />I was afraid this storytelling technique wouldn’t appeal to me or that I’d have a difficult time remembering everything and everyone, but the author manages to tell each story flawlessly and she’s able to perfectly connect all the stories. I cared about so many of the characters in each historical period and place. I enjoyed every single sub-story, and I became emotionally involved with each one.<br /><br />Right away, as soon as I opened the book, I was happy because there’s a map. A map is on the inside front and back covers. Oh, how I love maps in books! This one is wonderful because it follows the route the Hagggadah in the story took: from Seville in 1480 to Sarajevo in the twentieth century. There are a few drawings on the map of how things would have looked in these places/times too.<br /><br />I particularly enjoyed the story of present day Hanna’s relationship with her mother, but I hate to single out that one because there were so many wonderful characters and relationships. I was extremely touched by one of the main characters from the 1480 portion of the story.<br /><br />On page 320 of the book this quote: “The point – that diverse cultures influence and enrich one another…” sums up a lot of what happens during the course of the 500 years of the story. While this influence was shown not always to be from benign relationships, I found it particularly interesting and heartwarming that positive relationships between Jews and Muslims were shown throughout these 500 years. If there are any villains at all, it is the Christian inquisitors and rulers during the late 1400s to the early 1600s, but Christian, Muslim, and Jewish characters from all periods are shown as admirable, and often as having friendly and mutually beneficial relationships with one another. <br /><br />Both laudable and monstrous human characteristics are shown. There are people who risk their lives to save people and books; there is torture, slavery, and other atrocities as well. <br /><br />As someone who loves books, and who appreciates old books, I found this fictional history of a book fascinating. I was also absorbed both by the inside look at the craft of book conservation work and by the detective work that can be involved as part of it.<br /><br />On a personal note: I’ve been an Olympics junkie since my teens and clearly remember the Sarajevo Olympics, including the interviews at the time about how everyone in the area was getting along so well together, and then what happened there in the years following, so those parts of the story seemed very familiar to me.<br /><br />Historical fiction stories often torment me because I always want to know what’s real and what is fiction. As I was finishing up reading this, I planned to research what was history and what was fictional in this tale. What I very much appreciated about this book is that the author gives the reader all this information in the afterword; she does so in a very few pages but does so comprehensively.<br /><br />An added note: My book club meeting to discuss this book isn't for over a month, but I'm not concerned about it remembering enough; this is a memorable book.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17185529907210619829" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2098860464" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2098860464" />
    <a title="Joy D" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/50297885-joy-d"><img alt="Joy D" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1450639623p2/50297885.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2098860464?book_show_action=true">Feb 21, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joy D" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joy D" href="/user/show/50297885-joy-d">Joy D</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/12112072-people-of-the-book">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=religion">religion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=social-commentary">social-commentary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=books-about-books">books-about-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=europe">europe</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=art">art</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=zck">zck</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2098860464" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1879807282488417915">Multiple timeline historical fiction based upon the Sarajevo Haggadah, an elaborately illustrated ancient Judaic text that had been saved in the past by two Muslims and a Catholic priest. It is unusual in featuring artwork, which was not common in Jewish texts of the time period. The modern story follows Australian book conservator Hanna Heath who, in 1996, is called upon to evaluate and restore the book. During the evaluation, she finds small pieces of evidence of the book’s 500-year history. S</span>
  <span id="freeText1879807282488417915" style="display:none">Multiple timeline historical fiction based upon the Sarajevo Haggadah, an elaborately illustrated ancient Judaic text that had been saved in the past by two Muslims and a Catholic priest. It is unusual in featuring artwork, which was not common in Jewish texts of the time period. The modern story follows Australian book conservator Hanna Heath who, in 1996, is called upon to evaluate and restore the book. During the evaluation, she finds small pieces of evidence of the book’s 500-year history. She travels the world to consult with experts to discover its secrets. The modern story is interspersed with chapters showing how the book passed through the hands of people who created it, used it in Passover rituals, safeguarded it, and rescued it from destruction. Hands are mentioned numerous times and are clearly symbolic of their various attributes such as skill, purpose, blessing, expression, protection, justice, and humanitarianism. <br /><br />Brooks has obviously done extensive historical research, as the book includes episodes (and the associated brutality) of Spain in the 1400’s, the Spanish Inquisition, 19th century Vienna, World War II, and the Bosnian War. Sections of this book will appeal to bibliophiles, especially the detailed information about book-binding, preservation, old parchments, and the like. The past stories are filled with colorful well-drawn characters and vivid settings. Each historic vignette introduces new people and the reader should be prepared for multiple shifts in timeline and perspective. The book’s diverse backstories were more appealing to me than Hanna’s personal account, which at times ventured close to melodrama in the form of a rather unconvincing romance and a quarrelsome relationship with her insensitive mother.<br /><br />In the Afterword, the author clarifies what parts of this book are based on fact and what she has invented. The Sarajevo Haggadah is a real text dating back to medieval Spain and was saved by a Muslim museum curator during the Bosnian War. Unfortunately, not enough of its history is known to write a non-fiction account. Instead, the known history forms a framework for the author’s imagination. <br /><br />Overall, I think the author succeeds in exploring the emotional lives of her characters, showing human responses to traumatic events, and promoting tolerance among diverse faiths and ethnicities. If you appreciate books about books or the history of medieval artifacts, you will likely enjoy this book. <br /><br />Memorable quotes:<br />“With a few keystrokes of the hand, we touch the minds of many.”<br /><br />“The point – that diverse cultures influence and enrich one another – was made with silent eloquence.” <br /><br />“It [the Haggadah] was here to test us, to see if there were people who could see that what united us was more than what divided us.”</span>
  <a data-text-id="1879807282488417915" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_21673236" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/21673236" />
    <a title="Rachel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1024837-rachel"><img alt="Rachel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1206537365p2/1024837.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/21673236?book_show_action=true">May 05, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rachel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rachel" href="/user/show/1024837-rachel">Rachel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>
